Miscellaneous Records

Chahar Province's Road Construction Plan 319

— Xue Qian

Chahar Province borders Outer Mongolia and is a vital route to Kulun. Its automobile industry has been flourishing. The most notable routes are the Zhangku Line, from Zhangjiakou to Kulun, about 2,700 li long, and the Zhangduo Line, from Zhangjiakou to Duolun, about 600 li long. Because the grasslands are vast and boundless, without mountains or rivers to obstruct them, and with few engineering challenges, even the old Jingzhang Highway (from the old capital Beijing to Zhangjiakou), about 400 li long, which was used by automobiles during military times, has fallen into disrepair due to its parallel route with the PingSui Railway. No cars travel on it now. Since the autumn of the 17th year of the Republic (1928), Chahar Province has suffered from severe drought, making relief efforts difficult. In order to provide relief to the disaster victims, the authorities adopted a work-for-relief method, leading to a province-wide road network plan and the formulation of construction methods, which were submitted to the provincial government for record and circulated to all counties for implementation. In the 18th year, the Chahar Construction Department organized survey teams to conduct on-site surveys of important trunk lines. By the autumn of that year, most counties had started construction. By the end of the 19th year, the Yiwu Road, the first phase of the trunk line, had been completed. If road funds are available, it will not be difficult to gradually realize the plan. Recently, ten construction methods for the province have been drafted, demonstrating Chahar Province's commitment to road construction. Two key points of the road network construction methods are detailed below.

Provincial Road Network: According to the plan proposed by the Chahar Construction Department, it is divided into four phases. The first phase consists of trunk roads: (1) the Xuanwei Line, from Xuanhua via Shenjinghua and Shaoying towns to Xiheying in Wei County, 178 li long; (2) the Xuandu Line, from Xuanhua via Longchi counties to Dushi, 270 li long; (3) the Dugu Line, from Dushikou to Guyuan, the first line for entering Mongolia from the interior, 60 li long. The second phase consists of main lines: (1) the Yanghua Line, from Yangyuan County to Huashaoying, connecting with the Yiwei Line, 80 li long; (2) the Xinhua Line, from Huashaoying via Huai'an County, crossing the Chaigoubao of the PingSui Railway, along to Xinpingkou, the second line for entering Mongolian banners from the interior; (3) the Zhuoyuan Line, from Zhuolu to the Xiahuayuan Station of the PingSui Railway, 35 li long; (4) the Kangkang Line, from the Kangzhuang Station of the PingSui Railway to Yanqing County, 40 li long. The third phase consists of two branch lines: (1) the Zhangkang Branch Line, from Zhangbei County to Kangbao County, 200 li long; (2) the Fashang Branch Line, from Fayuan via Baochang and Kangbao counties to Shangqiu County, 450 li long. The fourth phase consists of four branch lines: (1) the Zhuowei Branch Line, from Zhuolu via Taohuabao, Xiheying, and Wei County to Guangling County in Shanxi, 230 li long; (2) the Longhuai Branch Line, from Longguan to Tumu, connecting with the Pinghai Road, 100 li long; (3) the Weixian Branch Line, from Wei County to Yangyuan County, 90 li long; (4) the Yangzhen Branch Line, from Yangyuan to Tianzhen in Shanxi, 90 li long. In general, the total length of automobile roads in Chahar Province is 2,013 li, including 853 li of trunk roads and 1,165 li of branch roads. There are two major rivers within the province, the Sanggan River and the Yang River, so there are six large bridges, requiring significant funding. The estimated construction cost for the province's roads is over 3.61 million yuan. Although the construction is divided into four phases, the completion date is undetermined due to the lack of funding. Only 120,000 yuan of disaster relief funds were used in the 19th year to complete the bridges, culverts, and stonework of the Xuanwei County. There should be several bridges and culverts un-repaired on the road. Construction will continue this spring. This road will soon be open to traffic along the entire line.

Construction Methods: The Jiangsu Construction Department has drafted ten rules for road construction: (1) The standards for the construction of Jiangsu provincial roads. The outline has nine points: A. Road surface width. Trunk roads should be 8 meters or more, and branch roads should be 6 meters or more as the standard. Drainage ditches and tree planting areas should be left 2 meters on each side. The wider, the better, if the land is easy to acquire. In addition to various vehicles, a 5-meter sidewalk should also be planned on both sides. The road surface material should first be a solid and firm sandy soil road. Later, observe the traffic situation and change to a gravel road. The center line of the road should be higher than the two sides of the road. One foot to one and a half feet is the standard. C. Where the route has uneven elevations, it must be filled or dug low. The slope on both sides is set to one foot high. Open one foot or one and a half feet diagonally. It must be determined according to the geology. D. Slope. In rugged mountainous areas, the maximum slope shall not exceed 6%. E. Roadbed construction. It must be laid flat layer by layer. The original shall not exceed eight inches. After one layer is evenly laid, lay the second layer until it reaches a certain height. F. Direction. The direction of the road is most valuable to take a straight and flat line. When it is impossible, bend it to keep it flat. Don't take the largest slope of narrow and shallow roads. G. Curvature. For the convenience and safety of road maintenance and transportation, the minimum radius is limited to 100 meters. H. Ditches. Drainage ditches should be built on both sides of the road. The width of the bottom is one foot, and the slope is one hundred to one. At the foot of the mountain, it depends on the size of the mountain. I. Bridges and culverts. Must be determined according to the size of the water flow. Should be made of cement or wood and stone to take a once-and-for-all plan and avoid driving danger. (2) The surveying of the entire province's road lines and the drafting of bridge and culvert drawings shall be the responsibility of personnel assigned by the Bureau. (3) The cost of repairing bridges and culverts on the province's roads shall be borne by the provincial treasury. (4) The earthwork involved in repairing roads, such as digging and filling soil, shall be the responsibility of all the people of the counties through which the route passes. Villages near the construction site should provide labor, and villages far away should provide money. The distribution of work and the standard for providing money should be discussed and drafted by the county governments in conjunction with various agencies and reported for record. (5) Civilian workers who undertake road construction will only be given food. They may be paid in cash, and the amount shall be determined by each county based on its own circumstances. However, the maximum shall not exceed 20 cents per worker. (6) Civilian workers' tools. Ordinary tools are generally provided by the civilians themselves, except for special tools borrowed from the public. (7) Civilian workers undertaking road construction shall be under the full responsibility of the county government's construction bureau for supervision and engineering. The Bureau shall also send personnel to inspect at any time. In case of difficult and important work, it shall be investigated at any time. (8) The privately owned land to be occupied for the construction of motorways in each county of this province shall be rented permanently in accordance with the provisions of Article 4 of the promulgated Land Requisition Law. The method will be determined separately. (9) If there are any matters not covered in these measures, they may be submitted to the provincial government for amendment at any time. (10) These measures shall be promulgated and implemented after being approved by the Provincial Government.

Paris as a World Automobile Market 321

— Xu

Although French people are easily squeezed out by competitors in the world automobile market, their capital, Paris, is often located in the center of the world automobile market.

Since the advent of automobiles, Paris has been at the forefront of the novelty of automobiles because of the organization of automobile exhibitions. This exhibition has been established for more than 25 years. Each meeting is not only a grand celebration of its magnificence and beauty, but also extremely demonstrates its position as the owner in the automobile market. Although foreign automobile manufacturers are well aware of this intention and want to replace it, it is obvious that they cannot. Therefore, for each meeting, they have to attend in person. Therefore, the types of automobiles exhibited at the venue are almost all in the world. This is why the Paris Motor Show is called the World Motor Show. As for automobile exhibitions everywhere, such as London, Berlin, and New York, only the products of automobile manufacturers in those places are displayed. Few foreigners participate. This year's New York Motor Show is the same. Only one French company participated. Looking back at the recent Paris Motor Show, it cannot be mentioned on the same day. The enthusiasm of foreigners to participate is no less than that of the French themselves. The reason is that the psychology of the majority of the audience recognizes Paris as an advanced country in the world automobile industry. Exhibiting each car will definitely be pleasing. Therefore, they do not spare the long journey and join hands to see the world's products of the year to refresh their senses. The custom is like this. Therefore, automobile manufacturers in various countries have no choice but to participate in order to compete for business. Moreover, the cars that are exhibited must be carefully designed. They value it as such. During the exhibition, automobile manufacturers use their skills to sell their factory products to private customers, car dealers, or car sales companies. Even more valuable, the Paris Motor Show can influence automobile manufacturers all over the world. Even if their manufacturing plans have been determined, not determined, or are in progress this year, they must re-determine their manufacturing steps after the exhibition closes to avoid any unsold products. This is why the Paris Motor Show is worthy of being called the world automobile market.

Book and Newspaper Introductions

India in National Revolutionary Struggle 322

— Wu Zhannong

Translated by Li Enshao and Wu Han

Today, amid the valiant national revolutionary struggle of India and the violent oppression of British imperialism, all who care about the self-determination of the world's small and weak nations丨丨especially those who advocate "uniting the world's small and weak nations in a common struggle"丨丨should have a thorough understanding of India's domestic political trends and diplomatic international relations. The forthcoming book "India in National Revolutionary Struggle" can be regarded as the best reference book for studying India's political situation.

India's national movement originated very early, but influenced by Buddhist scriptures and philosophy and imbued with Islamic theory, the thinking of the Indian people has largely tended towards conservatism and reconciliation. However, having been cleansed by revolutionary theory, the thinking of progressive youth is no less advanced than that of the youth in democratic political countries around the world. Therefore, although the political movement in India, even until this year丨丨1931丨丨on New Year's Day, has not yet achieved the realization of autonomy or independence, their spirit of struggle is vastly different from the time the National Congress was established in 1885. Their spirit of sacrifice, firm will, and sustained struggle have already been admired by Korea and Annam and conquered by imperialists all over the world.

India's vast territory, the differences in religion, people, and language (South Indians do not understand the language of North Indians and even use English as their national language), and the divergence of opinions due to different thoughts and the proliferation of factions, on the surface, it seems that the opinions of various parties, ethnic groups, and religions in India cannot be reconciled, and there is almost no one who does not hold a pessimistic view of India. However, upon careful observation, the future of India's political movement is entirely optimistic because, although their opinions are not consistent, some thoughts are too conservative, and even Gandhi, whom Indians regard as sacred, cannot avoid it, they are all standing on the position of resisting the yoke of British imperialism and striving sincerely for the freedom of India.

China and India are both ancient Eastern countries with many similarities in culture. In today's world, they are both among the countries being invaded. Before the concessions are recovered and the unequal treaties are abolished, China still cannot escape the status of a semi-colony or semi-colonial land. Our national struggle has not yet reached the goal of victory. We have the necessity of contacting and helping India's national revolution and the responsibility of fighting together. There is an urgent need to study India's political situation.

This book is written by William I. Hull Ph. th, Professor of International Relations and Doctor of Philosophy at Swarthmore Collnge. The original name is The Politiiar Crises of India, the latest edition published by Oxford Printing Company in 1930. The book does not divide chapters and sections, but only outlines and explains the process of various events, the attitude of the people, the public opinion of the society, and the countermeasures of the British government in chronological order, from the time of the establishment of the National Congress in 1985 to 1929. Among them, the political concepts of various religions in India, the virtue and spirit of various leaders, and the excitement of young people's thoughts are all expressed in fluent writing, or by borrowing the speeches or declarations of various leaders, the editorials and records of various newspapers, and indirectly and directly describe everything without omission. The main points and disputes of each meeting are especially thoroughly elaborated. The British government's violent acts of oppression are even more vividly portrayed, making it a unprecedented work for studying the Indian national revolution.

Translators Li Enshao and Wu Han have studied society, politics, and economics and are particularly familiar with the political parties and revolutionary histories of various countries. This time, they used their vivid writing to translate Mr. Ho's fluent writing, which made them shine even more brightly. Anyone who has read this book can clearly understand India's national revolution, whether it is the British government's evolutionary policy of aggression or the spirit of the Indian people's struggle for independence. It is specially introduced to the revolutionary readership of our country for reference.

A Brief History of Germany, written by Zheng Shoulian and published by Zhonghua Book Company, begins with the journey from China to Germany and covers various things, statistics, historical sites, language, education, national character, climate, celebrities, ancient events, and the research of German scholars on China, etc., listing everything without omission and quite pertinent. The entire book is divided into twenty-five chapters, each with illustrations, totaling forty or fifty. At the end of the book, there is also an index of various reference books.

Cooperation Monthly Legislative Special Issue, China Cooperative Society Cooperation Monthly, Volume 3, Issues 3 and 4 combined, is a legislative special issue with the following main items: "General Theory of Cooperative Laws," by Wang Shiying; "Essentials of British Cooperative Law," by Duanmu Zhuzhou; "Overview of German Cooperative Law," by He Qing; "On Swiss Cooperative Law," by Wang Shiying; "French Law on Cooperatives," by Chen Zhongming; "The Legal Status of Danish Cooperatives," by He Qing; "Soviet Cooperative Laws," by Hou Zhetang; "Belgian Cooperative Law," by Cheng Junqing; "Japanese Cooperative Law," by Wang Shiying; "A Brief Account of Indian Cooperative Law," by Wang Shiying; "Finnish Cooperative Law," by Cheng Junqing; (Appendix) Provisional Regulations for Rural Cooperatives. The European War Diary of French General Foch 325

— Translated by Gu Angruo

On April 25th, a meeting was held in Sarcus, and an immediate consensus was reached on this point. Commander Bliss also attended the meeting. After detailed discussions, it was decided in principle that in May and June, the American army would first dispatch infantry. The issue of May could be said to be completely resolved at that time. Regarding the issue of June, it was agreed at that time to make concrete decisions later. However, from now on, the Washington government is preparing to transport at least six divisions of infantry to France.

This meeting also highlighted the urgent need for the American army's command and deployment to benefit the Allied forces. The American army's command must be adaptable, and its deployment must avoid individual control. This was agreed upon by General Pershing and Marquis Milner. The April 25th meeting further proved this. In summary, the Allied governments will comprehensively study the strength of the American army in future military matters. All resolutions are generally related to the transportation of the American army.

This was decided at the Cibef Conference on May 1st and 2nd. The content is as follows:

(1) The British government will be responsible for providing ships to transport 130,000 American soldiers to France in May and 150,000 in June, all of whom are infantry and machine gun units.

(2) American ships will be used to transport artillery and other troops.

(3) The situation will be studied again in early June to decide on future plans.

This important resolution was successfully achieved thanks to the generous cooperation of British ships. By July 1st, no less than 400,000 American infantry and machine gun troops had been transported to France to fill the void in the British and French armies. At the same time, Commander Pershing used American ships to transport other troops continuously. Because he was able to form an independent American army in France in a short period of time, special interests and the common interests of the Allied countries were taken into account.

If limited to the United States' own capabilities, the American troops transported in March 1918 were only 80,000. In April, there were 93,000. With British assistance, this number surged to 240,000 in May and 280,000 in June.

However, with the arrival of a large American army, other problems arose. The American army did not have time to complete its training in the country, so it had to continue in France. The training problem was easy to solve, but the army's organization and the supply of weapons needed for the new army were quite complicated. To avoid errors, shortages, and delays, close attention must be paid at all times.

My staff specially set up a department in Paris, under the command of the Assistant Chief of Staff, to specialize in collecting and managing various issues related to completing the training of the American army.

The German offensive in Flanders came to an end on May 9th. On the 18th, Commander Mitry was ordered to reduce the strength of his front lines in order to strengthen his reserves, as mentioned earlier. However, the military officer of Kik was also ordered to appropriately reduce the flood defenses to alleviate the inconvenience to residents.

On March 21st, the German bombardment indicated the start of a major battle, preceded by a period of calm, the likes of which had not been seen for a year. What was hidden behind this calm? We knew that the enemy had a large force at his disposal. According to the intelligence department of the General Headquarters, the enemy had 76 to 80 divisions, which was more than he used in the first offensive between the Chemin des Dames and the Arras River. Where would the large enemy army launch its attack? This mystery of the war situation was difficult to unravel.

Commander-in-Chief Haig believed that the Germans were in danger of launching a new offensive between Amiens and the North Sea, with the main target being an attack on Arras or Hazebrouck. Commander Pétain also had the same observation. He was certainly no longer afraid that the enemy would attack in Champagne. Because of the lack of other evidence, he did not believe the prisoners captured on May 19th and 22nd who said that the Germans were preparing to launch a major offensive between the Arras River and Reims.

I do not advocate that the Allied forces' actions should be limited to speculating and discussing the enemy's plans. Another urgent task that we should pay attention to is the preparation of our army to take the offensive. "Only by taking the offensive can our army win. We must strike first to revive our military prestige."

But while our army is busy replenishing its strength, the results of this offensive battle must be commensurate with the sacrifices made. There are two war zones that meet this condition:

(1) The area between the Arras River and the Somme River. This area would allow the French Third and First Armies to jointly attack with the British Second Army, with the goal of relieving the siege of the Paris-Amiens railway line and the transportation hub of Amiens.

(2) The Lys River area. This area could be attacked by the left wing of the British First Army and the northern detachment, with the goal of driving out the enemy that was pressing on Ypres and the Béthune coal mining area.

Having chosen these two operational areas, the Allied forces' offensive plan was issued to the commanders-in-chief on May 20th.

Preparations for the offensive in both places were stepped up at the same time. If the enemy did not attack, the Allied forces' attacks in both places could catch the enemy off guard. If the enemy attacked, our army's attack could also become a valuable counterattack. In terms of troop strength, the first attack, between the Arras River and the Somme River, was certainly the most

Home
